colspan="2">\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Nile</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">&#1575;&#1604;&#1606;&#1610;&#1604;</span>&#8206;, written as al-N&#299;l; pronounced as an-N&#299;l) is a major north-flowing <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">river</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northeast_Africa" title="Northeast Africa">northeastern Africa</a>, and is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_by_length" title="List of rivers by length">longest river</a> in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica-nile_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-nile-2">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu-etal-river-length_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-liu-etal-river-length-3">[2]</a></sup> though some sources cite the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River" title="Amazon River">Amazon River</a> as the longest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmazonRiver_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmazonRiver-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica-amazon-length_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-amazon-length-5">[4]</a></sup> The Nile, which is about 6,650&#160;km (4,130&#160;mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-length_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-length-1">[n 1]</a></sup> long, is an "international" river as its <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> covers eleven countries, namely, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Sudan" title="Republic of the Sudan">Republic of the Sudan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> In particular, the Nile is the primary water source of Egypt and Sudan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hdl.handle_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-hdl.handle-8">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The river Nile has two major <a href="/wiki/Tributary" title="Tributary">tributaries</a>, the <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile" title="Blue Nile">Blue Nile</a>. The White Nile is considered to be the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Headwaters" title="Headwaters">headwaters</a> and primary stream of the Nile itself. The Blue Nile, however, is the source of most of the <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">silt</a>. The White Nile is longer and rises in the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> region of central Africa, with the most distant source still undetermined but located in either Rwanda or Burundi. It flows north through Tanzania, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a>, Uganda and South Sudan.  The Blue Nile begins at <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tana" title="Lake Tana">Lake Tana</a> in Ethiopia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> and flows into Sudan from the southeast. The two rivers meet just north of the Sudanese capital of <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The northern section of the river flows north almost entirely through the Sudanese <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a> to Egypt, then ends in <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">a large delta</a> and flows into the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>. <a href="/wiki/History_of_Egypt" title="History of Egypt">Egyptian civilization</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sudan" title="History of Sudan">Sudanese kingdoms</a> have depended on the river since ancient times. Most of the population and cities of Egypt lie along those parts of the Nile <a href="/wiki/Valley" title="Valley">valley</a> north of <a href="/wiki/Aswan" title="Aswan">Aswan</a>, and nearly all the cultural and historical sites of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> are found along river banks.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Eonile</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Integrated_Nile"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Role in the founding of Egyptian civilization</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Search_for_the_source_of_the_Nile"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Search for the source of the Nile</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Since_1950"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Since 1950</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Water_sharing_dispute"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Water sharing dispute</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Modern_achievements_and_exploration"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Modern achievements and exploration</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#White_Nile"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">White Nile</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Blue_Nile_2"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Blue Nile</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Crossings"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Crossings</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Crossings_from_Khartoum_to_the_Mediterranean_Sea"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Crossings from Khartoum to the Mediterranean Sea</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Crossings_from_Jinja,_Uganda_to_Khartoum"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Crossings from Jinja, Uganda to Khartoum</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Gallery"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Gallery</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Annotated_bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Annotated bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Notes_and_references"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Notes and references</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_names">Etymology and names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology and names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In the ancient <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian language</a>, the Nile is called <i><a class="external text" href="http://medu.ipetisut.com/index.php?og=nile" rel="nofollow">&#7716;\'p&#299;</a></i> or <i><a class="external text" href="http://medu.ipetisut.com/index.php?og=river" rel="nofollow">Iteru</a></i> (Hapy), meaning "river". In <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>, the word <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E2%B2%93%E2%B2%81%E2%B2%A3%E2%B2%9F" title="wikt:&#11411;&#11393;&#11427;&#11423;">&#11435;&#11411;&#11393;&#11427;&#11441;</a>, pronounced <i>piaro</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahidic" title="Sahidic">Sahidic</a>) or <i>phiaro</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bohairic" title="Bohairic">Bohairic</a>), means "the river" (lit. p(h).iar-o "the.canal-great"), and comes from the same ancient name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hillel_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hillel-11">[10]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Egyptian Arabic">Egyptian Arabic</a>, the Nile is called <i>en-N&#299;l</i> while in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standard_Arabic" title="Standard Arabic">Standard Arabic</a> it is called <i>an-N&#299;l</i>. In <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Biblical Hebrew</a>: <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r867359662">.mw-parser-output .script-hebrew,.mw-parser-output .script-Hebr{font-size:1.15em;font-family:"Ezra SIL","Ezra SIL SR","Keter Aram Tsova","Taamey Ashkenaz","Taamey David CLM","Taamey Frank CLM","Frank Ruehl CLM","Keter YG","Shofar","David CLM","Hadasim CLM","Simple CLM","Nachlieli","SBL BibLit","SBL Hebrew",Cardo,Alef,"Noto Serif Hebrew","Noto Sans Hebrew","David Libre",David,"Times New Roman",Gisha,Arial,FreeSerif,FreeSans}</style><span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%;">&#1492;&#1463;&#1497;&#1456;&#1488;&#1493;&#1465;&#1512;</span>&#8236;, <i>Ha-Ye\'or</i> or <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r867359662" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%;">&#1492;&#1463;&#1513;&#1460;&#1473;&#1497;&#1495;&#1493;&#1465;&#1512;</span>&#8236;, <i>Ha-Shi&#7717;or</i>.\n</p><p>The English name <i>Nile</i> and the Arabic names <i>en-N&#238;l</i> and <i>an-N&#238;l</i> both derive from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i><i lang="la" title="Latin language text">Nilus</i></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#925;&#949;&#8150;&#955;&#959;&#962;</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB-name_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB-name-13">[12]</a></sup> Beyond that, however, the etymology is disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB-name_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB-name-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a> at his <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i> refers that <a href="/wiki/Nilus_(mythology)" title="Nilus (mythology)">Nilus</a> (&#925;&#949;&#8150;&#955;&#959;&#962;) was one of the <a href="/wiki/Potamoi" title="Potamoi">Potamoi</a> (river gods), son of <a href="/wiki/Oceanus" title="Oceanus">Oceanus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(mythology)" title="Tethys (mythology)">Tethys</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> Another derivation of <i>Nile</i> might be related to the term <i>Nil</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa">&#2344;&#2368;&#2354;</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Sanskrit" title="Romanization of Sanskrit">translit.</a>&#160;</small><i lang="sa-Latn" title="Sanskrit-language transliteration"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/nila" title="wikt:nila">nila</a></i>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic_language" title="Egyptian Arabic language">Egyptian Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="arz">&#1606;&#1610;&#1604;&#1577;</span>&#8206;),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hillel_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hillel-11">[10]</a></sup> which refers to <a href="/wiki/Indigofera_tinctoria" title="Indigofera tinctoria">Indigofera tinctoria</a>, one of the original sources of <a href="/wiki/Indigo_dye" title="Indigo dye">indigo dye</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Nymphaea_caerulea" title="Nymphaea caerulea">Nymphaea caerulea</a>, known as "The Sacred Blue Lily of the Nile", which was found scattered over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tutankhamen" title="Tutankhamen">Tutankhamen</a>&#8217;s corpse when it was located in 1922.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another possible etymology derives it from a <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic</a> <i>Nahal</i>, meaning "river".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> The standard English names "White Nile" and "Blue Nile", to refer to the river\'s source, derive from <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> names formerly applied only to the Sudanese stretches which meet at Khartoum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Courses">Courses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Courses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nile_SPOT_1173.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Nile_SPOT_1173.jpg/170px-Nile_SPOT_1173.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Nile_SPOT_1173.jpg/255px-Nile_SPOT_1173.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Nile_SPOT_1173.jpg/340px-Nile_SPOT_1173.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nile_SPOT_1173.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Nile at <a href="/wiki/Dendera" title="Dendera">Dendera</a>, as seen from the <a href="/wiki/SPOT_(satellite)" title="SPOT (satellite)">SPOT</a> satellite</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nile_basin_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="396" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Nile_basin_map.png/220px-Nile_basin_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Nile_basin_map.png/330px-Nile_basin_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Nile_basin_map.png/440px-Nile_basin_map.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nile_basin_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Nile\'s <a href="/wiki/Drainage_divide" title="Drainage divide">watershed</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nile2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Nile2.jpg/220px-Nile2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Nile2.jpg/330px-Nile2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Nile2.jpg/440px-Nile2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nile2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Nile near <a href="/wiki/Beni_Suef" title="Beni Suef">Beni Suef</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nile_composite_NASA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2325" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="988" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Nile_composite_NASA.jpg/170px-Nile_composite_NASA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Nile_composite_NASA.jpg/255px-Nile_composite_NASA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Nile_composite_NASA.jpg/340px-Nile_composite_NASA.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nile_composite_NASA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Composite_image" title="Composite image">Composite</a> satellite image of the White Nile</div></div></div>\n<p>With a total length of about 6,650&#160;km (4,130&#160;mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-length_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-length-1">[n 1]</a></sup> between the region of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, the Nile is the longest river on the African continent. The <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> of the Nile covers 3,254,555 square kilometers (1,256,591&#160;sq&#160;mi), about 10% of the area of Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> The Nile basin is complex, and because of this, the discharge at any given point along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mainstem_(hydrology)" title="Mainstem (hydrology)">mainstem</a> depends on many factors including weather, diversions, evaporation and <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> flow.\n</p><p>Above <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a>, the Nile is also known as the <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a>, a term also used in a limited sense to describe the section between <a href="/wiki/Lake_No" title="Lake No">Lake No</a> and Khartoum. At Khartoum the river is joined by the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile" title="Blue Nile">Blue Nile</a>. The White Nile starts in equatorial East Africa, and the Blue Nile begins in Ethiopia. Both branches are on the western flanks of the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Rift" title="East African Rift">East African Rift</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Source_(river_or_stream)" title="Source (river or stream)">source</a> of the Nile  is sometimes considered to be Lake Victoria, but the lake has feeder rivers of considerable size. The <a href="/wiki/Kagera_River" title="Kagera River">Kagera River</a>, which flows into Lake Victoria near the Tanzanian town of <a href="/wiki/Bukoba" title="Bukoba">Bukoba</a>, is the longest feeder, although sources do not agree on which is the longest tributary of the Kagera and hence the most distant source of the Nile  itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> It is either the <a href="/wiki/Ruvyironza_River" title="Ruvyironza River">Ruvyironza</a>, which emerges in <a href="/wiki/Bururi_Province" title="Bururi Province">Bururi Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nyabarongo" title="Nyabarongo">Nyabarongo</a>, which flows from <a href="/wiki/Nyungwe_Forest" title="Nyungwe Forest">Nyungwe Forest</a> in Rwanda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> The two feeder rivers meet near <a href="/wiki/Rusumo_Falls" title="Rusumo Falls">Rusumo Falls</a> on the Rwanda-Tanzania border.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Source_of_Nile,_Spring_at_Jinja,_Lake_Victoria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1877" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Source_of_Nile%2C_Spring_at_Jinja%2C_Lake_Victoria.jpg/220px-Source_of_Nile%2C_Spring_at_Jinja%2C_Lake_Victoria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Source_of_Nile%2C_Spring_at_Jinja%2C_Lake_Victoria.jpg/330px-Source_of_Nile%2C_Spring_at_Jinja%2C_Lake_Victoria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Source_of_Nile%2C_Spring_at_Jinja%2C_Lake_Victoria.jpg/440px-Source_of_Nile%2C_Spring_at_Jinja%2C_Lake_Victoria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Source_of_Nile,_Spring_at_Jinja,_Lake_Victoria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The source of the Nile from an underwater spring at the neck of Lake Victoria, Jinja</div></div></div>\n<p>In 2010, an exploration party<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> went to a place described as the source of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rukarara" title="Rukarara">Rukarara</a> tributary,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> and by hacking a path up steep jungle-choked mountain slopes in the Nyungwe forest found (in the <a href="/wiki/Dry_season" title="Dry season">dry season</a>) an appreciable incoming surface flow for many kilometres upstream, and found a new source, giving the Nile a length of 6,758&#160;km (4,199&#160;mi).\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gish_Abay" title="Gish Abay">Gish Abay</a> is reportedly the place where the "holy water" of the first drops of the Blue Nile develop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="In_Uganda.27s_Nile"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Uganda\'s_Nile">In Uganda\'s Nile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: In Uganda\'s Nile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Nile  leaves Lake Nyanza (Victoria) at <a href="/wiki/Ripon_Falls" title="Ripon Falls">Ripon Falls</a> near <a href="/wiki/Jinja,_Uganda" title="Jinja, Uganda">Jinja, Uganda</a>, as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victoria_Nile" title="Victoria Nile">Victoria Nile</a>. It flows north for some 130 kilometers (81&#160;mi), to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Kyoga" title="Lake Kyoga">Lake Kyoga</a>. The last part of the approximately 200 kilometers (120&#160;mi) river section starts from the western shores of the lake and flows at first to the west until just south of <a href="/wiki/Masindi_Port" title="Masindi Port">Masindi Port</a>, where the river turns north, then makes a great half circle to the east and north until <a href="/wiki/Karuma_Falls" title="Karuma Falls">Karuma Falls</a>. For the remaining part it flows merely westerly through the <a href="/wiki/Murchison_Falls" title="Murchison Falls">Murchison Falls</a> until it reaches the very northern shores of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Albert_(Africa)" title="Lake Albert (Africa)">Lake Albert</a> where it forms a significant <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">river delta</a>. The lake itself is on the border of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DR_Congo" title="DR Congo">DR Congo</a>, but the Nile  is not a border river at this point. After leaving Lake Albert, the river continues north through Uganda and is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albert_Nile" title="Albert Nile">Albert Nile</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span id="In_South_Sudan.27s_Nile"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="In_South_Sudan\'s_Nile">In South Sudan\'s Nile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: In South Sudan\'s Nile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Nile river flows into <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> just south of <a href="/wiki/Nimule" title="Nimule">Nimule</a>, where it is known as the <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">Bahr al Jabal</a> ("Mountain River"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup>). Just south of the town it has the <a href="/wiki/Confluence" title="Confluence">confluence</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Achwa_River" title="Achwa River">Achwa River</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bahr_el_Ghazal_River" title="Bahr el Ghazal River">Bahr al Ghazal</a>, itself 716 kilometers (445&#160;mi) long, joins the Bahr al Jabal at a small lagoon called <a href="/wiki/Lake_No" title="Lake No">Lake No</a>, after which the Nile becomes known as the <i>Bahr al Abyad</i>, or the <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a>, from the whitish <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> suspended in its waters. When the <a href="/wiki/Flooding_of_the_Nile" title="Flooding of the Nile">Nile floods</a> it leaves a rich silty deposit which fertilizes the soil. The Nile no longer floods in Egypt since the completion of the <a href="/wiki/Aswan_Dam" title="Aswan Dam">Aswan Dam</a> in 1970. An <a href="/wiki/Anabranch" title="Anabranch">anabranch</a> river, the <a href="/wiki/Bahr_el_Zeraf" title="Bahr el Zeraf">Bahr el Zeraf</a>, flows out of the Nile\'s Bahr al Jabal section and rejoins the White Nile.\n</p><p>The flow rate of the Bahr al Jabal at <a href="/wiki/Mongalla,_South_Sudan" title="Mongalla, South Sudan">Mongalla, South Sudan</a> is almost constant throughout the year and averages 1,048&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/s (37,000&#160;cu&#160;ft/s). After Mongalla, the Bahr Al Jabal enters the enormous swamps of the <a href="/wiki/Sudd" title="Sudd">Sudd</a> region of South Sudan. More than half of the Nile\'s water is lost in this swamp to <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a>. The average flow rate of the White Nile at the tails of the swamps is about 510&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/s (18,000&#160;cu&#160;ft/s). From here it soon meets with the <a href="/wiki/Sobat_River" title="Sobat River">Sobat River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Malakal" title="Malakal">Malakal</a>. On an annual basis, the White Nile upstream of Malakal contributes about fifteen percent of the total outflow of the Nile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The average flow of the White Nile at Lake Kawaki Malakal, just below the Sobat River, is 924&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/s (32,600&#160;cu&#160;ft/s); the peak flow is approximately 1,218&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/s (43,000&#160;cu&#160;ft/s) in October and minimum flow is about 609&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/s (21,500&#160;cu&#160;ft/s) in April. This fluctuation is due to the substantial variation in the flow of the Sobat, which has a minimum flow of about 99&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/s (3,500&#160;cu&#160;ft/s) in March and a peak flow of over 680&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/s (24,000&#160;cu&#160;ft/s) in October.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> During the dry season (January to June) the White Nile contributes between 70 percent and 90 percent of the total discharge from the Nile.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Sudan">In Sudan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: In Sudan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Below <a href="/wiki/Renk,_South_Sudan" title="Renk, South Sudan">Renk</a> the White Nile enters Sudan, it flows north to Khartoum and meets the Blue Nile.\n</p><p>The course of the Nile in Sudan is distinctive. It flows over <a href="/wiki/Cataracts_of_the_Nile" title="Cataracts of the Nile">six groups of cataracts</a>, from the sixth at <a href="/wiki/Sabaloka_Game_Reserve" title="Sabaloka Game Reserve">Sabaloka</a> just north of <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a> northward to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Hamed" title="Abu Hamed">Abu Hamed</a>. Due to the tectonic uplift of the <a href="/wiki/Nubian_Swell" title="Nubian Swell">Nubian Swell</a>, the river is then diverted to flow for over 300&#160;km south-west following the structure of the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Shear_Zone" title="Central African Shear Zone">Central African Shear Zone</a> embracing the <a href="/wiki/Bayuda_Desert" title="Bayuda Desert">Bayuda Desert</a>. At <a href="/wiki/Al_Dabbah,_Sudan" title="Al Dabbah, Sudan">Al Dabbah</a> it resumes its northward course towards the first Cataract at <a href="/wiki/Aswan" title="Aswan">Aswan</a> forming the \'S\'-shaped Great Bend of the Nile<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> already mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the north of Sudan the river enters <a href="/wiki/Lake_Nasser" title="Lake Nasser">Lake Nasser</a> (known in Sudan as Lake Nubia), the larger part of which is in Egypt.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Egypt">In Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: In Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Below the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aswan_High_Dam" title="Aswan High Dam">Aswan High Dam</a>, at the northern limit of Lake Nasser, the Nile resumes its historic course.\n</p><p>North of <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, the Nile splits into two branches (or <a href="/wiki/Distributary" title="Distributary">distributaries</a>) that feed the Mediterranean: the <a href="/wiki/Rosetta" title="Rosetta">Rosetta</a> Branch to the west and the <a href="/wiki/Damietta" title="Damietta">Damietta</a> to the east, forming the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tributaries_of_Nile">Tributaries of Nile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Tributaries of Nile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atbara_River">Atbara River</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Atbara River">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Below the confluence with the Blue Nile the only major tributary is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atbara_River" title="Atbara River">Atbara River</a>, roughly halfway to the sea, which originates in Ethiopia north of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tana" title="Lake Tana">Lake Tana</a>, and is around 800 kilometers (500&#160;mi) long. The Atbara flows only while there is rain in Ethiopia and dries very rapidly. During the dry period of January to June, it typically dries up north of Khartoum.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blue_Nile">Blue Nile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Blue Nile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile" title="Blue Nile">Blue Nile</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5246" data-file-width="7869" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg/220px-ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg/330px-ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg/440px-ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ET_Bahir_Dar_asv2018-02_img17_Tis_Issat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile_Falls" title="Blue Nile Falls">Blue Nile Falls</a> fed by <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tana" title="Lake Tana">Lake Tana</a> near the city of <a href="/wiki/Bahir_Dar" title="Bahir Dar">Bahir Dar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg/220px-Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg/330px-Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg/440px-Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nile_River_and_delta_from_orbit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a> from space</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ISS036-E-011050.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/ISS036-E-011050.jpg/220px-ISS036-E-011050.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/ISS036-E-011050.jpg/330px-ISS036-E-011050.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/ISS036-E-011050.jpg/440px-ISS036-E-011050.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ISS036-E-011050.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Annotated view of the Nile and Red Sea, with a dust storm<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile" title="Blue Nile">Blue Nile</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amharic_language" title="Amharic language">Amharic</a>: <span lang="am">&#4819;&#4707;&#4845;</span>, <i>&#703;&#256;bay</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup>) springs\nfrom <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tana" title="Lake Tana">Lake Tana</a> in the Ethiopian Highlands. The Blue Nile flows about 1,400 kilometres to Khartoum, where the Blue Nile and White Nile join to form the Nile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> Ninety percent of the water and ninety-six percent of the transported sediment carried by the Nile<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> originates in Ethiopia, with fifty-nine percent of the water from the Blue Nile (the rest being from the <a href="/wiki/Tekez%C3%A9_River" title="Tekez&#233; River">Tekez&#233;</a>, Atbarah, <a href="/wiki/Sobat_River" title="Sobat River">Sobat</a>, and small tributaries). The erosion and transportation of silt only occurs during the Ethiopian rainy season in the summer, however, when rainfall is especially high on the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Highlands" title="Ethiopian Highlands">Ethiopian Plateau</a>; the rest of the year, the great rivers draining Ethiopia into the Nile (Sobat, Blue Nile, Tekez&#233;, and Atbarah) have a weaker flow. In harsh and arid seasons and droughts the blue Nile dries out completely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The flow of the Blue Nile varies considerably over its yearly cycle and is the main contribution to the large natural variation of the Nile flow. During the dry season the natural discharge of the Blue Nile can be as low as 113&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/s (4,000&#160;cu&#160;ft/s), although upstream dams regulate the flow of the river. During the wet season the peak flow of the Blue Nile often exceeds 5,663&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/s (200,000&#160;cu&#160;ft/s) in late August (a difference of a factor of 50).\n</p><p>Before the placement of dams on the river the yearly discharge varied by a factor of 15 at Aswan. Peak flows of over 8,212&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/s (290,000&#160;cu&#160;ft/s) occurred during late August and early September, and minimum flows of about 552&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/s (19,500&#160;cu&#160;ft/s) occurred during late April and early May.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bahr_el_Ghazal_and_Sobat_River">Bahr el Ghazal and Sobat River</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Bahr el Ghazal and Sobat River">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bahr_el_Ghazal_River" title="Bahr el Ghazal River">Bahr al Ghazal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sobat_River" title="Sobat River">Sobat River</a> are the two most important tributaries of the White Nile in terms of discharge.\n</p><p>The Bahr al Ghazal\'s <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> is the largest of any of the Nile\'s sub-basins, measuring 520,000 square kilometers (200,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) in size, but it contributes a relatively small amount of water, about 2&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/s (71&#160;cu&#160;ft/s) annually, due to tremendous volumes of water being lost in the Sudd wetlands.\n</p><p>The Sobat River, which joins the Nile a short distance below Lake No, drains about half as much land, 225,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (86,900&#160;sq&#160;mi), but contributes 412 cubic meters per second (14,500&#160;cu&#160;ft/s) annually to the Nile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> When in flood the Sobat carries a large amount of sediment, adding greatly to the White Nile\'s color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Yellow_Nile">Yellow Nile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Yellow Nile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Yellow Nile is a former tributary that connected the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ouadda%C3%AF_Highlands" title="Ouadda&#239; Highlands">Ouadda&#239; Highlands</a> of eastern <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> to the Nile River Valley c. 8000 to c. 1000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> Its remains are known as the <a href="/wiki/Wadi_Howar" title="Wadi Howar">Wadi Howar</a>. The wadi passes through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gharb_Darfur" title="Gharb Darfur">Gharb Darfur</a> near the northern border with Chad and meets up with the Nile near the southern point of the Great Bend.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sahara#Climate_history" title="Sahara">Sahara &#167;&#160;Climate history</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herodotus_world_map-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="476" data-file-width="788" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/220px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/330px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/440px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herodotus_world_map-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reconstruction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oikoumene" title="Oikoumene">Oikoumene</a> (inhabited world), an ancient map based on <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a>\' description of the world, circa 450 BC</div></div></div>\n<p>The Nile  (<i>iteru</i> in <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Ancient Egyptian</a>) has been the lifeline of civilization in Egypt since the <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a>, with most of the population and all of the cities of Egypt resting along those parts of the Nile valley lying north of Aswan. However, the Nile used to run much more westerly through what is now Wadi Hamim and Wadi al Maqar in <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> and flow into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Sidra" title="Gulf of Sidra">Gulf of Sidra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> As sea level rose at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">most recent ice age</a>, the stream which is now the northern Nile <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_capture" title="River capture">pirated</a> the ancestral Nile near <a href="/wiki/Asyut" title="Asyut">Asyut</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> this change in climate also led to the creation of the current <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> desert, around 3400 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eonile">Eonile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Eonile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The present Nile is at least the fifth river that has flowed north from the Ethiopian Highlands. <a href="/wiki/Satellite_imagery" title="Satellite imagery">Satellite imagery</a> was used to identify dry watercourses in the desert to the west of the Nile. An Eonile canyon, now filled by surface drift, represents an ancestral Nile called the <b>Eonile</b> that flowed during the later <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> (23&#8211;5.3 million years before present). The Eonile transported <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clastic" title="Clastic">clastic sediments</a> to the Mediterranean; several natural gas fields have been discovered within these sediments.\n</p><p>During the late-Miocene <a href="/wiki/Messinian_salinity_crisis" title="Messinian salinity crisis">Messinian salinity crisis</a>, when the Mediterranean Sea was a closed basin and evaporated to the point of being empty or nearly so, the Nile cut its course down to the new base level until it was several hundred metres below world ocean level at Aswan and 2,400&#160;m (7,900&#160;ft) below Cairo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warren2006_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warren2006-45">[44]</a></sup> This created a very long and deep canyon which was filled with sediment when the Mediterranean was recreated. At some point the sediments raised the riverbed sufficiently for the river to overflow westward into a depression to create <a href="/wiki/Lake_Moeris" title="Lake Moeris">Lake Moeris</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a> drained northwards into the Nile until the <a href="/wiki/Virunga_Mountains" title="Virunga Mountains">Virunga Volcanoes</a> blocked its course in Rwanda. The Nile was much longer at that time, with its furthest headwaters in northern <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Integrated_Nile">Integrated Nile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Integrated Nile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>There are two theories about the age of the integrated Nile. One is that the integrated drainage of the Nile is of young age, and that the Nile basin was formerly broken into series of separate basins, only the most northerly of which fed a river following the present course of the Nile in Egypt and Sudan. Rushdi Said postulated that Egypt itself supplied most of the waters of the Nile during the early part of its history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Said1981_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Said1981-46">[45]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The other theory is that the drainage from Ethiopia via rivers equivalent to the Blue Nile, the Atbara and the <a href="/wiki/Tekez%C3%A9_River" title="Tekez&#233; River">Takazze</a> flowed to the Mediterranean via the Egyptian Nile since well back into <a href="/wiki/Tertiary" title="Tertiary">Tertiary</a> times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WW1980_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-WW1980-47">[46]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Salama suggested that during the <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neogene" title="Neogene">Neogene</a> Periods (66 million to 2.588 million years ago) a series of separate closed continental basins each occupied one of the major parts of the Sudanese Rift System: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mellut_rift" title="Mellut rift">Mellut rift</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_Nile_rift" title="White Nile rift">White Nile rift</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile_rift" title="Blue Nile rift">Blue Nile rift</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Atbara_rift&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Atbara rift (page does not exist)">Atbara rift</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sag_El_Naam_rift&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sag El Naam rift (page does not exist)">Sag El Naam rift</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salama1987_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salama1987-48">[47]</a></sup>\nThe Mellut Rift Basin is nearly 12 kilometers (7.5&#160;mi) deep at its central part. This rift is possibly still active, with reported <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic" title="Tectonic">tectonic</a> activity in its northern and southern boundaries. The <a href="/wiki/Sudd" title="Sudd">Sudd swamps</a> which form the central part of the basin may still be subsiding. The White Nile Rift System, although shallower than the <a href="/wiki/Bahr_el_Arab_rift" title="Bahr el Arab rift">Bahr el Arab rift</a>, is about 9 kilometers (5.6&#160;mi) deep. Geophysical exploration of the Blue Nile Rift System estimated the depth of the sediments to be 5&#8211;9 kilometers (3.1&#8211;5.6&#160;mi). These basins were not interconnected until their subsidence ceased, and the rate of sediment deposition was enough to fill and connect them. The Egyptian Nile connected to the Sudanese Nile, which captures the Ethiopian and Equatorial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Headwater" title="Headwater">headwaters</a> during the current stages of tectonic activity in the Eastern, Central and Sudanese Rift Systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Salama1997_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Salama1997-49">[48]</a></sup> The connection of the different Niles occurred during cyclic wet periods. The River Atbara overflowed its closed basin during the wet periods that occurred about 100,000 to 120,000 years ago. The Blue Nile connected to the main Nile during the 70,000&#8211;80,000 years B.P. wet period. The White Nile system in Bahr El Arab and White Nile Rifts remained a closed lake until the connection of the Victoria Nile to the main system some 12,500 years ago during the <a href="/wiki/African_humid_period" title="African humid period">African humid period</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_the_founding_of_Egyptian_civilization">Role in the founding of Egyptian civilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Role in the founding of Egyptian civilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2595" data-file-width="1817" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg/170px-Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg/255px-Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg/340px-Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dhows_on_the_Nile.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Felucca" title="Felucca">felucca</a> traversing the Nile near Aswan</div></div></div>\n<p>The Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> wrote that "Egypt was the gift of the Nile". An unending source of sustenance, it provided a crucial role in the development of Egyptians civilization. Silt deposits from the Nile made the surrounding land fertile because the river overflowed its banks annually. The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egyptians</a> cultivated and traded <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a> and other crops around the Nile. Wheat was a crucial crop in the famine-plagued Middle East. This trading system secured Egypt\'s diplomatic relationships with other countries, and contributed to economic stability. Far-reaching trade has been carried on along the Nile since ancient times. A tune, <a href="/wiki/Hymn_to_the_Nile" title="Hymn to the Nile">Hymn to the Nile</a>, was created and sung by the ancient Egyptian peoples about the flooding of the Nile River and all of the miracles it brought to Ancient Egyptian civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-50">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestic_buffalo" title="Domestic buffalo">Water buffalo</a> were introduced from Asia, and Assyrians introduced <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camels</a> in the 7th century BC. These animals were killed for meat, and were domesticated and used for ploughing&#8212;or in the camels\' case, carriage. Water was vital to both people and livestock. The Nile was also a convenient and efficient means of transportation for people and goods.\nThe Nile was an important part of ancient Egyptian spiritual life. <a href="/wiki/Hapi_(Nile_god)" title="Hapi (Nile god)">Hapi</a> was the god of the annual floods, and both he and the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaoh</a> were thought to control the flooding. The Nile was considered to be a causeway from life to death and the afterlife. The east was thought of as a place of birth and growth, and the west was considered the place of death, as the god <a href="/wiki/Ra" title="Ra">Ra</a>, the Sun, underwent birth, death, and resurrection each day as he crossed the sky. Thus, all tombs were west of the Nile, because the Egyptians believed that in order to enter the afterlife, they had to be buried on the side that symbolized death.\n</p><p>As the Nile was such an important factor in Egyptian life, the ancient calendar was even based on the 3 cycles of the Nile. These seasons, each consisting of four months of thirty days each, were called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akhet_(hieroglyph_and_season)" title="Akhet (hieroglyph and season)">Akhet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Season_of_the_Emergence" title="Season of the Emergence">Peret</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shemu" title="Shemu">Shemu</a>. Akhet, which means inundation, was the time of the year when the Nile flooded, leaving several layers of fertile soil behind, aiding in agricultural growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpringerMorris2010_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpringerMorris2010-51">[50]</a></sup> Peret was the growing season, and Shemu, the last season, was the harvest season when there were no rains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpringerMorris2010_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-SpringerMorris2010-51">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Search_for_the_source_of_the_Nile">Search for the source of the Nile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Search for the source of the Nile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JH_Speke.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="772" data-file-width="604" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/JH_Speke.jpg/170px-JH_Speke.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/JH_Speke.jpg/255px-JH_Speke.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/JH_Speke.jpg/340px-JH_Speke.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JH_Speke.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Hanning_Speke" title="John Hanning Speke">John&#160;Hanning&#160;Speke</a>&#160;c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1863</span>. Speke was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victorian_Age" title="Victorian Age">Victorian</a> explorer who first reached <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> in 1858, returning to establish it as the source of the Nile by 1862.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911698_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911698-52">[51]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1433" data-file-width="911" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg/170px-Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg/255px-Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg/340px-Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Morton_Stanley_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henry_Morton_Stanley" title="Henry Morton Stanley">Henry Morton Stanley</a> in 1872. Stanley circumnavigated the lake and confirmed Speke\'s observations in 1875.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911698_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911698-52">[51]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Owing to their failure to penetrate the <a href="/wiki/Sudd" title="Sudd">sudd</a> wetlands of <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>, the upper reaches of the Nile remained largely unknown to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greeks" title="Ancient Greeks">ancient Greeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Romans</a>. Various expeditions failed to determine the river\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Source_(river_or_stream)" title="Source (river or stream)">source</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agatharcides" title="Agatharcides">Agatharcides</a> records that in the time of <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_II_Philadelphus" title="Ptolemy II Philadelphus">Ptolemy II Philadelphus</a>, a military expedition had penetrated far enough along the course of the Blue Nile to determine that the summer floods were caused by heavy seasonal rainstorms in the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Highlands" title="Ethiopian Highlands">Ethiopian Highlands</a>, but no European of antiquity is known to have reached <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tana" title="Lake Tana">Lake Tana</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tabula_Rogeriana" title="Tabula Rogeriana">Tabula Rogeriana</a> depicted the source as three lakes in 1154.\n</p><p>Europeans began to learn about the origins of the Nile in the 14th century when the Pope sent monks as emissaries to Mongolia who passed India, the middle east and Africa, and described being told of the source of the Nile in Abyssinia (ancient European name for Ethiopia)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-THE_TRAVELS_OF_JOHN_DE_MARIGNOLLI_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-THE_TRAVELS_OF_JOHN_DE_MARIGNOLLI-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> Later in the 15th and 16th centuries, travelers to Ethiopia visited Lake Tana and the source of the Blue Nile in the mountains south of the lake. Although <a href="/wiki/James_Bruce" title="James Bruce">James Bruce</a> claimed to be the first European to have visited the headwaters,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> modern writers give the credit to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> <a href="/wiki/Pedro_P%C3%A1ez" title="Pedro P&#225;ez">Pedro P&#225;ez</a>. P&#225;ez\'s account of the source of the Nile<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> is a long and vivid account of Ethiopia. It was published in full only in the early 20th century, although it was featured in works of P&#225;ez\'s contemporaries, including Baltazar T&#233;llez,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Athanasius_Kircher" title="Athanasius Kircher">Athanasius Kircher</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> and by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Michael_Vansleb" title="Johann Michael Vansleb">Johann Michael Vansleb</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Europeans had been resident in Ethiopia since the late 15th century, and one of them may have visited the headwaters even earlier without leaving a written trace. The Portuguese Jo&#227;o Bermudes published the first description of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tis_Issat" title="Tis Issat">Tis Issat</a> Falls in his 1565 memoirs, compared them to the Nile Falls alluded to in <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>\'s <i>De Republica</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jer%C3%B3nimo_Lobo" title="Jer&#243;nimo Lobo">Jer&#243;nimo Lobo</a> describes the source of the Blue Nile, visiting shortly after Pedro P&#225;ez. Telles also used his account.\n</p><p>The White Nile was even less understood. The ancients mistakenly believed that the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a> represented the upper reaches of the White Nile. For example, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> wrote that the Nile had its origins "in a mountain of lower <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a>", flowed above ground for "many days" distance, then went underground, reappeared as a large lake in the territories of the <a href="/wiki/Masaesyli" title="Masaesyli">Masaesyli</a>, then sank again below the desert to flow underground "for a distance of 20 days\' journey till it reaches the nearest Ethiopians."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> A merchant named Diogenes reported that the Nile\'s water attracted game such as buffalo.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1911Nile.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1310" data-file-width="652" decoding="async" height="402" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/1911Nile.png/200px-1911Nile.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/1911Nile.png/300px-1911Nile.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/1911Nile.png/400px-1911Nile.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1911Nile.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of the Nile c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1911</span>, a time when its entire primary course ran through British occupations, condominiums, colonies, and protectorates<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911693_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChisholm1911693-62">[61]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Lake Victoria was first sighted by Europeans in 1858 when the British <a href="/wiki/List_of_explorers" title="List of explorers">explorer</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Hanning_Speke" title="John Hanning Speke">John Hanning Speke</a> reached its southern shore while traveling with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Francis_Burton" title="Richard Francis Burton">Richard Francis Burton</a> to explore central Africa and locate the great lakes. Believing he had found the source of the Nile on seeing this "vast expanse of open water" for the first time, Speke named the lake after the then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victoria_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Victoria of the United Kingdom">Queen of the United Kingdom</a>. Burton, recovering from illness and resting further south on the shores of Lake Tanganyika, was outraged that Speke claimed to have proved his discovery to be the true source of the Nile when Burton regarded this as still unsettled. A very public quarrel ensued, which sparked a great deal of intense debate within the scientific community and interest by other explorers keen to either confirm or refute Speke\'s discovery. British explorer and missionary <a href="/wiki/David_Livingstone" title="David Livingstone">David Livingstone</a> pushed too far west and entered the <a href="/wiki/Congo_River" title="Congo River">Congo River</a> system instead. It was ultimately Welsh-American explorer <a href="/wiki/Henry_Morton_Stanley" title="Henry Morton Stanley">Henry Morton Stanley</a> who confirmed Speke\'s discovery, circumnavigating Lake Victoria and reporting the great outflow at <a href="/wiki/Ripon_Falls" title="Ripon Falls">Ripon Falls</a> on the Lake\'s northern shore.\n</p><p>European involvement in Egypt goes back to the time of <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laird_Shipyard" title="Laird Shipyard">Laird Shipyard</a> of <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> sent an iron steamer to the Nile in the 1830s. With the completion of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> and the British takeover of Egypt in the 1870s, more British river steamers followed.\n</p><p>The Nile is the area\'s natural navigation channel, giving access to Khartoum and Sudan by steamer. The <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Khartoum" title="Siege of Khartoum">Siege of Khartoum</a> was broken with purpose-built <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sternwheelers" title="Sternwheelers">sternwheelers</a> shipped from England and steamed up the river to retake the city. After this came regular steam navigation of the river. With British Forces in Egypt in the First World War and the inter-war years, river steamers provided both security and sightseeing to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giza_Necropolis" title="Giza Necropolis">Pyramids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Egypt" title="Thebes, Egypt">Thebes</a>. Steam navigation remained integral to the two countries as late as 1962. Sudan steamer traffic was a lifeline as few railways or roads were built in that country. Most paddle steamers have been retired to shorefront service, but modern diesel tourist boats remain on the river.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Village_on_the_Nile,_1891.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1458" data-file-width="1945" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Village_on_the_Nile%2C_1891.jpg/220px-Village_on_the_Nile%2C_1891.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Village_on_the_Nile%2C_1891.jpg/330px-Village_on_the_Nile%2C_1891.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Village_on_the_Nile%2C_1891.jpg/440px-Village_on_the_Nile%2C_1891.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Village_on_the_Nile,_1891.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Village on the Nile, 1891</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Since_1950">Since 1950</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Since 1950">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KageraRuvubu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/KageraRuvubu.jpg/220px-KageraRuvubu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/KageraRuvubu.jpg/330px-KageraRuvubu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/KageraRuvubu.jpg/440px-KageraRuvubu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KageraRuvubu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The confluence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kagera_river" title="Kagera river">Kagera</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruvubu_river" title="Ruvubu river">Ruvubu</a> rivers near <a href="/wiki/Rusumo_Falls" title="Rusumo Falls">Rusumo Falls</a>, part of the Nile\'s upper reaches</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nile03(js).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1276" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Nile03%28js%29.jpg/220px-Nile03%28js%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Nile03%28js%29.jpg/330px-Nile03%28js%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Nile03%28js%29.jpg/440px-Nile03%28js%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nile03(js).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhows" title="Dhows">Dhows</a> on the Nile</div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1331" data-file-width="1718" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg/220px-View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg/330px-View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg/440px-View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:View_from_Cairo_Tower_31march2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Nile passes through Cairo, Egypt\'s capital city.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Nile has long been used to transport goods along its length. Winter winds blow south, up river, so ships could sail up river, and down river using the flow of the river.\nWhile most Egyptians still live in the Nile valley, the 1970 completion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aswan_High_Dam" title="Aswan High Dam">Aswan High Dam</a> ended the summer floods and their renewal of the fertile soil, fundamentally changing farming practices. The Nile supports much of the population living along its banks, enabling Egyptians to live in otherwise inhospitable regions of the Sahara. The rivers\'s flow is disturbed at several points by the <a href="/wiki/Cataracts_of_the_Nile" title="Cataracts of the Nile">Cataracts of the Nile</a>, which are sections of faster-flowing water with many small islands, shallow water, and rocks, which form an obstacle to navigation by boats. The <a href="/wiki/Sudd" title="Sudd">Sudd</a> wetlands in Sudan also forms a formidable navigation obstacle and impede water flow, to the extent that Sudan had once attempted to canalize (the <a href="/wiki/Jonglei_Canal" title="Jonglei Canal">Jonglei Canal</a>) to bypass the swamps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nile cities include Khartoum, Aswan, <a href="/wiki/Luxor" title="Luxor">Luxor</a> (<a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Egypt" title="Thebes, Egypt">Thebes</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Giza" title="Giza">Giza</a>&#160;&#8211; <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a> conurbation. The first cataract, the closest to the mouth of the river, is at Aswan, north of the Aswan Dam. This part of the river is a regular tourist route, with cruise ships and traditional wooden sailing boats known as <a href="/wiki/Felucca" title="Felucca">feluccas</a>. Many cruise ships ply the route between Luxor and Aswan, stopping at <a href="/wiki/Edfu" title="Edfu">Edfu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kom_Ombo" title="Kom Ombo">Kom Ombo</a> along the way. Security concerns have limited cruising on the northernmost portion for many years.\n</p><p>A computer simulation study to plan the economic development of the Nile was directed by H.A.W. Morrice and W.N. Allan, for the Ministry of Hydro-power of the Republic of the Sudan, during 1955&#8211;1957<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> Morrice was their Hydrological Adviser, and Allan his predecessor. M.P. Barnett directed the software development and computer operations. The calculations were enabled by accurate monthly inflow data collected for 50 years. The underlying principle was the use of over-year storage, to conserve water from rainy years for use in dry years. Irrigation, navigation and other needs were considered. Each computer run postulated a set of reservoirs and operating equations for the release of water as a function of the month and the levels upstream. The behavior that would have resulted given the inflow data was modeled. Over 600 models were run. Recommendations were made to the Sudanese authorities. The calculations were run on an IBM 650 computer. Simulation studies to design water resources are discussed further in the article on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrology_transport_model" title="Hydrology transport model">hydrology transport models</a>, that have been used since the 1980s to analyze water quality.\n</p><p>Despite the development of many reservoirs, drought during the 1980s led to widespread starvation in Ethiopia and Sudan, but Egypt was nourished by water impounded in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Nasser" title="Lake Nasser">Lake Nasser</a>. Drought has proven to be a major cause of fatality in the Nile river basin. According to a report by the Strategic Foresight Group around 170 million people have been affected by droughts in the last century with half a million lives lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_Peace_for_the_Nile,_2009_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_Peace_for_the_Nile,_2009-68">[67]</a></sup> From the 70 incidents of drought which took place between 1900 and 2012, 55 incidents took place in Ethiopia, Sudan, South Sudan, Kenya and Tanzania.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blue_Peace_for_the_Nile,_2009_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blue_Peace_for_the_Nile,_2009-68">[67]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_sharing_dispute">Water sharing dispute</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Water sharing dispute">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Nile\'s water has affected the politics of East Africa and the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> for many decades. Countries including Uganda, Sudan, Ethiopia and Kenya have complained about Egyptian domination of its water resources. The <a href="/wiki/Nile_Basin_Initiative" title="Nile Basin Initiative">Nile Basin Initiative</a> promotes a peaceful cooperation among those states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several attempts have been made to establish agreements between the countries sharing the Nile waters. It is very difficult to have all these countries agree with each other given the self-interest of each country and their political, strategic, and social differences. On 14 May 2010 at <a href="/wiki/Entebbe" title="Entebbe">Entebbe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> signed a new agreement on sharing the Nile water even though this agreement raised strong opposition from Egypt and Sudan. Ideally, such international agreements should promote equitable and efficient usage of the Nile basin\'s water resources. Without a better understanding about the availability of the future water resources of the Nile, it is possible that conflicts could arise between these countries relying on the Nile for their water supply, economic and social developments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hdl.handle_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-hdl.handle-8">[7]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_achievements_and_exploration">Modern achievements and exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Modern achievements and exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="White_Nile">White Nile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: White Nile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1951, the American <a href="/wiki/John_Goddard_(adventurer)" title="John Goddard (adventurer)">John Goddard</a> together with two French explorers became the first to successfully navigate the entire Nile river from its source in Burundi at the potential <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Headsprings&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Headsprings (page does not exist)">headsprings</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Kagera_River" title="Kagera River">Kagera River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a> to its mouth on the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, a journey of approximately 6,800&#160;km (4,200&#160;mi). Their 9-month journey is described in the book &#8216;Kayaks down the Nile&#8217;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a> Expedition, led by <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South African</a> national <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Coetzee" title="Hendrik Coetzee">Hendrik Coetzee</a>, navigated the White Nile\'s entire length of approximately 3,700 kilometres (2,300&#160;mi). The expedition began at the White Nile\'s beginning at <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> in <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, on 17 January 2004 and arrived safely at the Mediterranean in <a href="/wiki/Rosetta" title="Rosetta">Rosetta</a>, four and a half months later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> On the 30th of April 2005 a team led by South Africans Peter Meredith and Hendrik Coetzee, following again in the footsteps of John Goddard, navigated the major remote source of the White Nile, the <a href="/wiki/Kagera_River" title="Kagera River">Akagera river</a> that starts as the <a href="/wiki/Ruvyironza_River" title="Ruvyironza River">Ruvyironza</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bururi_Province" title="Bururi Province">Bururi Province</a>, Burundi, and ends at Lake Victoria, Uganda.\n</p><p>In April 2006, the Ascend the Nile Expedition including two explorers from Britain and one from New Zealand ascended the river from its mouth at <a href="/wiki/Rosetta" title="Rosetta">Rosetta</a> to one of its sources in Rwanda\'s <a href="/wiki/Nyungwe_Forest" title="Nyungwe Forest">Nyungwe Forest</a>. The Team including Cam McLeay, Neil McGrigor and Garth MacIntyre spent 70 days travelling to the Rwandese source of the Nile covering approximately 6800 kilometres. During the Expedition they were ambushed by the LRA (Lord\'s Resistance Army) led by the notorious <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Kony" title="Joseph Kony">Joseph Kony</a>, however post-attack six months later they returned to complete the expedition. They measured the length of the river with the help of GPS and claimed to have found the furthest source.
