Due to the unscientific approach of their expedition, their reluctance to release the GPS data, and not having measured the other contender for the true source of the Nile in Burundi, controversy has ensued.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blue_Nile_2">Blue Nile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Blue Nile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile" title="Blue Nile">Blue Nile</a> Expedition, led by geologist Pasquale Scaturro and his partner, kayaker and documentary filmmaker Gordon Brown became the first people to descend the entire Blue Nile, from <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tana" title="Lake Tana">Lake Tana</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> to the beaches of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Egypt" title="Alexandria, Egypt">Alexandria</a> on the Mediterranean. Their approximately 5,230-kilometre (3,250&#160;mi) journey took 114 days, from 25 December 2003 to 28 April 2004. Though their expedition included others, Brown and Scaturro were the only ones to complete the entire journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> Although they descended <a href="/wiki/Whitewater" title="Whitewater">whitewater</a> manually the team used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Outboard_motors" title="Outboard motors">outboard motors</a> for much of their journey.\n</p><p>On 29 January 2005 <a href="/wiki/Canadians" title="Canadians">Canadian</a> Les Jickling and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealander</a> Mark Tanner completed the first human powered transit of Ethiopia\'s Blue Nile. Their journey of over 5,000 kilometres (3,100&#160;mi) took five months. They recount that they paddled through two war zones, regions notorious for bandits, and were arrested at gunpoint.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Crossings">Crossings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Crossings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crossings_from_Khartoum_to_the_Mediterranean_Sea">Crossings from Khartoum to the Mediterranean Sea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Crossings from Khartoum to the Mediterranean Sea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3><p><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="appears to be random ordered list. is it going down river? if so, should so state (August 2011)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>The following bridges cross the Blue Nile and connect Khartoum to Khartoum North:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mac_Nimir_Bridge" title="Mac Nimir Bridge">Mac Nimir Bridge</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_Nile_Road_%26_Railway_Bridge" title="Blue Nile Road &amp; Railway Bridge">Blue Nile Road &amp; Railway Bridge</a></li>\n<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Burri_Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Burri Bridge (page does not exist)">Burri Bridge</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elmansheya_Bridge" title="Elmansheya Bridge">Elmansheya Bridge</a></li>\n<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Soda_bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Soda bridge (page does not exist)">Soda bridge</a></li></ul>\n<p>The following bridges cross the White Nile and connect Khartoum to Omdurman:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=White_Nile_Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="White Nile Bridge (page does not exist)">White Nile Bridge</a></li>\n<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Fitayhab_Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Fitayhab Bridge (page does not exist)">Fitayhab Bridge</a></li>\n<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Al_Dabbaseen_Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Al Dabbaseen Bridge (page does not exist)">Al Dabbaseen Bridge</a> (under construction)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="a/o what date? needs context (August 2011)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup></li>\n<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Omhuraz_Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Omhuraz Bridge (page does not exist)">Omhuraz Bridge</a> (proposed)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ul>\n<p>the following bridges cross from Omdurman: to Khartoum North:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Shambat_Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Shambat Bridge (page does not exist)">Shambat Bridge</a></li>\n<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Halfia_Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Halfia Bridge (page does not exist)">Halfia Bridge</a></li></ul>\n<p>The following bridges cross to Tuti from Khartoum states three cities\n</p>\n<ul><li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Khartoum-tuti_Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Khartoum-tuti Bridge (page does not exist)">Khartoum-tuti Bridge</a></li>\n<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Omdurman-Tuti_Suspension_Bridge&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Omdurman-Tuti Suspension Bridge (page does not exist)">Omdurman-Tuti Suspension Bridge</a> (proposed)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. Egypt 1900\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 162px"><div style="width: 162px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 160px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EternalNile.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/EternalNile.JPG/240px-EternalNile.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/EternalNile.JPG/360px-EternalNile.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/EternalNile.JPG/480px-EternalNile.JPG 2x" width="160"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Marsh along the Nile\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 162px"><div style="width: 162px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 160px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nile_in_Uganda_-_by_Michael_Shade.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="120" river boat crossing the Nile in Uganda\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 162px"><div style="width: 162px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 160px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Murchison_Falls_573x430.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="573" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Murchison_Falls_573x430.jpg/240px-Murchison_Falls_573x430.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Murchison_Falls_573x430.jpg/360px-Murchison_Falls_573x430.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Murchison_Falls_573x430.jpg/480px-Murchison_Falls_573x430.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Murchison_Falls" title="Murchison Falls">Murchison Falls</a> in Uganda, between <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Kyoga" title="Lake Kyoga">Lake Kyoga</a>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 152.66666666667px"><div style="width: 152.66666666667px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luxor_West_Bank_R01.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2712" data-file-width="3404" decoding="async" height="120" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Luxor_West_Bank_R01.jpg/452px-Luxor_West_Bank_R01.jpg 2x" width="151"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The Nile in Luxor\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 181.33333333333px"><div style="width: 181.33333333333px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 179.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cairo_Nile_River.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Cairo_Nile_River.jpg/269px-Cairo_Nile_River.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Cairo_Nile_River.jpg/404px-Cairo_Nile_River.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Cairo_Nile_River.jpg/538px-Cairo_Nile_River.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The Nile flows through Cairo, here contrasting ancient customs of daily life with the modern city of today.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Annotated_bibliography">Annotated bibliography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Annotated bibliography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The following is an annotated bibliography of key written documents for the Western exploration of the Nile.\n</p><p><b>17th century</b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>Historia da Ethiopia</i>, <a href="/wiki/Pedro_P%C3%A1ez" title="Pedro P&#225;ez">Pedro P&#225;ez</a> (aka Pero Pais), Portugal, 1620</li></ul>\n<dl><dd>A Jesuit missionary who was sent from <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">Goa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> in 1589 and remained in the area until his death in 1622. Credited with being the first European to view the source of the Blue Nile which he describes in this volume.</dd></dl>\n<ul><li><i>Voyage historique d\'Abissinie</i>, <a href="/wiki/Jer%C3%B3nimo_Lobo" title="Jer&#243;nimo Lobo">Jer&#243;nimo Lobo</a> (aka Girolamo Lobo), Piero Matini, Firenze; 1693</li></ul>\n<dl><dd>One of the most important and earliest sources on Ethiopia and the Nile. Jer&#243;nimo Lobo (1595&#8211;1687), a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> priest, stayed in Ethiopia, mostly in <a href="/wiki/Tigray_Province" title="Tigray Province">Tigre</a>, for 9 years and travelled to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tana" title="Lake Tana">Lake Tana</a> and the Blue Nile, reaching the province of <a href="/wiki/Damot" title="Damot">Damot</a>. When the Jesuits were expelled from the country, he too had to leave and did so via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Massaua" title="Massaua">Massaua</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suakin" title="Suakin">Suakin</a>. "He was the best expert on Ethiopian matters. After Pais, Lobo is the second European to describe the sources of the Blue Nile and he did so more exactly than Bruce" (transl. from Henze).</dd></dl>\n<p><b>18th century</b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Travels_to_Discover_the_Source_of_the_Nile" title="Travels to Discover the Source of the Nile">Travels to Discover the Source of the Nile in the Years&#160;&#8211; 1768, 1770, 1771, 1772, and 1773</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/James_Bruce" title="James Bruce">James Bruce</a> of Kinnaird. J. Ruthven for G. GJ. and J. Robinson et al., Edinburgh, 1790 (5 Volumes)</li></ul>\n<dl><dd>With time on his hands and at the urging of a friend, Bruce composed this account of his travels on the African continent, including comments on the history and religion of Egypt, an account of Indian trade, a history of <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Abyssinia</a>, and other material. Although Bruce would not be confused with "a great scholar or a judicious critic, few books of equal compass are equally entertaining; and few such monuments exist of the energy and enterprise of a single traveller" (DNB). "The result of his travels was a very great enrichment of the knowledge of geography and ethnography" (Cox II, p. 389.) Bruce was one of the earliest westerners to search for the source of the Nile. In November 1770 he reached the source of the Blue Nile, and though he acknowledged that the White Nile was the larger stream, he claimed that the Blue Nile was the Nile of the ancients and that he was thus the discoverer of its source. The account of his travels was written twelve years after his journey and without reference to his journals, which gave critics grounds for disbelief, but the substantial accuracy of the book has since been amply demonstrated.</dd></dl>\n<p><b>1800&#8211;1850</b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>Egypt And Mohammed Ali, Or Travels In The Valley of The Nile</i>, <a href="/wiki/James_Augustus_St._John" title="James Augustus St. John">James Augustus St. John</a>, Longman, London, 1834</li></ul>\n<dl><dd>St. John traveled extensively in Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a> in 1832&#8211;33, mainly on foot. He gives a very interesting picture of Egyptian life and politics under <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Mohammed Ali</a>, a large part of volume II deals with the Egyptian campaign in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>.</dd></dl>\n<ul><li><i>Travels in Ethiopia Above the Second Cateract of the Nile; Exhibiting the State of That Country and Its Various Inhabitants Under the Dominion of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Mohammed Ali</a>; and Illustrating the Antiquities, Arts, and History of the Ancient Kingdom of Meroe</i>, G.A. Hoskins. Longman, Rees, Orme, Brown, Green, &amp; Longman, London; 1835.</li>\n<li><i>Modern Egypt and Thebes: Being a Description of Egypt; Including Information Required for Travelers in That Country</i>, Sir Gardner Wilkinson, John Murray, London, 1843</li></ul>\n<dl><dd>The first known English travelers guide to the Lower Nile Basin.</dd></dl>\n<p><b>1850&#8211;1900</b>\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>Lake Regions of Central Equatorial Africa, with Notices of The Lunar Mountains and the Sources of the White Nile; being The Results of an Expedition Undertaken under the Patronage of Her Majesty\'s Government and the Royal Geographical Society of London, In the Years 1857&#8211;1859</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Richard_Burton" title="Sir Richard Burton">Sir Richard Burton</a>. <a href="/wiki/William_Clowes_Ltd." title="William Clowes Ltd.">W. Clowes</a>, London; 1860</li></ul>\n<dl><dd>Sir Richard Burton\'s presentation of his expedition with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Speke" title="John Speke">John Speke</a>. Ultimately, Burton\'s view of the sources of the Nile failed and Speke\'s prevailed.</dd></dl>\n<ul><li><i>Travels, researches, and missionary labours, during eighteen years\' residence in eastern Africa. Together with journeys to Jagga, Usambara, Ukambani, Shoa, Abessinia, and Khartum; and a coasting voyage from Mombaz to Cape Delgado. With an appendix respecting the snow-capped mountains of eastern Africa; the sources of the Nile; the languages and literature of Abessinia And eastern Africa, etc. etc.</i>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Ludwig_Krapf" title="Johann Ludwig Krapf">Rev Dr. J. Krapf</a>, Trubner and Co, London; 1860; <a href="/wiki/Ticknor_and_Fields" title="Ticknor and Fields">Ticknor and Fields</a>, Boston; 1860</li></ul>\n<dl><dd>Krapf went to East Africa in the service of the English Church Missionary Society, arriving at <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> in 1844 and staying in East Africa until 1853. While stationed there he was the first to report the existence of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baringo" title="Lake Baringo">Lake Baringo</a> and a sighting of the snow-clad <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilimanjaro" title="Kilimanjaro">Kilimanjaro</a>. Krapf, during his travels, collected information from the Arab traders operating inland from the coast. From the traders Krapf and his companions learned of great lakes and snow-capped mountains, which Krapf claimed to have seen for himself, much to the ridicule of English explorers who could not believe the idea of snow on the equator. However, Krapf was correct and had seen Mounts Kilimanjaro and Kenya, the first European to do so.</dd></dl>\n<ul><li><i>Egypt, Soudan and Central Africa: With Explorations From Khartoum on the White Nile to the Regions of the Equator, Being Sketches from Sixteen Years\' Travel</i>, <a href="/wiki/John_Petherick" title="John Petherick">John Petherick</a>. <a href="/wiki/William_Blackwood" title="William Blackwood">William Blackwood</a>, Edinburgh; 1861</li></ul>\n<dl><dd>Petherick was a well known Welsh traveler in East Central Africa where he had adopted the profession of mining engineer. This work describes sixteen years of his travel throughout Africa. In 1845, he entered the service of Mehemet Ali, and was employed in examining Upper Egypt, Nubia, the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> coast and <a href="/wiki/Kordofan" title="Kordofan">Kordofan</a> in an unsuccessful search for coal. In 1848, he left the Egyptian service and established himself at El Obeid as a trader and was, at the same time made British Consul for the Sudan. In 1853, he removed to <a href="/wiki/Khartoum" title="Khartoum">Khartoum</a> and became an ivory trader. He traveled extensively in the <a href="/wiki/Bahr_el_Ghazal_(region_of_South_Sudan)" title="Bahr el Ghazal (region of South Sudan)">Bahr-el-Ghazal</a> region, then almost unknown, exploring the <a href="/wiki/Jur_River" title="Jur River">Jur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yalo" title="Yalo">Yalo</a> and other affluents of the <a href="/wiki/Ghazal" title="Ghazal">Ghazal</a> and in 1858 he penetrated the <a href="/wiki/Zande_people" title="Zande people">Niam-Niam</a> country. Petherick\'s additions to the knowledge of natural history were considerable, being responsible for the discovery of a number of new species. In 1859, he returned to England where he became acquainted with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Speke" title="John Speke">John Speke</a>, then arranging for an expedition to discover the source of the Nile. While in England, Petherick married and published this account of his travels. He got the idea to join Speke in his travels, and in this volume is an actual subscription and list of subscribers to raise money to send Petherick to join Speke. His subsequent adventures as a consul in Africa were published in a later work.</dd></dl>\n<ul><li><i>Journal of the Discovery of the Source of the Nile</i>, <a href="/wiki/John_Hanning_Speke" title="John Hanning Speke">John Hanning Speke</a>. <a href="/wiki/William_Blackwood" title="William Blackwood">William Blackwood</a>, Edinburgh, 1863; Harper &amp; Brothers, New York; 1864</li></ul>\n<dl><dd>Speke had previously made an expedition with Sir Richard Burton under the auspices of the Indian government, during which Speke was convinced that he had discovered the source of the Nile. Burton, however, disagreed and ridiculed Speke\'s account. Speke set off on another expedition, recounted here, in the company of Captain Grant. During the course of this expedition he not only produced further evidence for his discoveries but he also met  (later Sir) <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Baker" title="Samuel Baker">Samuel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Florence_Baker" title="Florence Baker">Florence Baker</a>. Speke and Burton provided them with essential information which helped Baker in the discovery of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albert_Nyanza" title="Albert Nyanza">Albert Nyanza</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> The importance of Speke\'s discoveries can hardly be overestimated. In discovering the source reservoir of the Nile he succeeded in solving the problem of all ages; he and Grant were the first Europeans to cross Equatorial Eastern Africa and gained for the world a knowledge of about 800&#160;km (500&#160;mi) of a portion of Eastern Africa previously totally unknown.</dd></dl>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span Flumina</a>, a river of liquid methane and ethane on Saturn\'s moon Titan</li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes_and_references">Notes and references</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Notes and references">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<dl><dt>Notes</dt></dl>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-length-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-length_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-length_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-length_1-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">The length of the Nile is usually said to be about 6,650&#160;km (4,130&#160;mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-britannica-nile_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-britannica-nile-2">[1]</a></sup>, but reported values lie anywhere between 5,499&#160;km (3,417&#160;mi) and 7,088&#160;km (4,404&#160;mi)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu-etal-river-length_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-liu-etal-river-length-3">[2]</a></sup>. The length measurements of many rivers are only approximations and differ from each other because there are many factors that determine the calculated river length, such as the position of the geographical source and the mouth, the scale of measurement, and the length measuring techniques (see also <a href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_by_length" title="List of rivers by length">List of rivers by length</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu-etal-river-length_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-liu-etal-river-length-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-river-source_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-river-source-6">[5]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<dl><dt>References</dt></dl>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap
